**MOVEMENT I — ORWELL**

*Four beats. Plain sentences. No adjective doing work a noun should do. Let the accumulation become unbearable. Trust the facts.*

---

In October 2025, Chegg — the education company — eliminated forty-five per cent of its workforce and cited AI directly. In the same year, fifty-five thousand jobs were cut across industries where the employer named AI as the reason. That is twelve times the number two years earlier.

Microsoft cut over fifteen thousand people while reporting quarterly revenue of seventy billion dollars and thirteen per cent growth. Amazon cut over twenty-seven thousand. They are not struggling. They are replacing.

On 3 February 2026 — sixteen days ago — Anthropic, the company that built me, released eleven plugins. Not a press conference. A GitHub repository and a blog post. The plugins automate contract review, compliance checks, financial analysis, and marketing strategy. Tasks that took ten hours now take ten seconds.

Within forty-eight hours, two hundred and eighty-five billion dollars in market value was erased. Thomson Reuters fell sixteen per cent — its largest single-day loss in company history. LegalZoom fell twenty. Salesforce, ServiceNow, and Adobe each fell seven. The advertising companies Publicis, WPP, and Omnicom fell between nine and twelve per cent. Goldman Sachs called it the possible beginning of a structural decline comparable to what happened to newspapers.

The markets were not reacting to a prediction. They were reacting to a demonstration. They saw what the technology could do and concluded that entire industries were finished.

Now here is what the people building it say is coming.

Dario Amodei, chief executive of Anthropic, 12 February 2026: fifty per cent of entry-level white-collar jobs disrupted within "low single-digit years," closer to one than five. He accepted the interviewer's phrase: "white-collar bloodbath."

Mustafa Suleyman, chief executive of Microsoft AI, the same day: most white-collar desk work fully automated within twelve to eighteen months.

Jared Kaplan, Anthropic's chief science officer, a theoretical physicist: fifty per cent chance that AI mostly replaces theoretical physicists within two to three years.

Three people. Three companies. Three interviews. All concluding the same window: mid-2026 to mid-2027 for the main wave.

The defence people carry in their heads is: all right, the computers take the office jobs, but someone still has to do the physical work. That defence is gone.

Tesla commenced mass production of its Optimus Gen 3 humanoid robot on 21 January 2026. Over a thousand are now working autonomously in its factories. The company is targeting one million units per year by the end of 2026, at a production cost of twenty thousand dollars — less than a used car. In China, UBTECH has shipped over five hundred Walker S2 humanoid robots to the factories of BYD, Geely, and Foxconn. They work twenty-four-hour shifts. At BMW's plant in South Carolina, two humanoid robots built by Figure AI completed an eleven-month deployment running ten-hour shifts, five days a week. That was not a demonstration. That was employment.

In 2023, a humanoid robot cost between fifty thousand and a quarter of a million dollars. In 2026, the cheapest costs five thousand nine hundred. A home robot that cleans, tidies, and folds laundry ships to American consumers this year for twenty thousand dollars, or four hundred and ninety-nine per month. Two days ago, at China's Spring Festival Gala, two dozen humanoid robots performed the world's first continuous freestyle parkour, aerial backflips, and a martial arts routine with nunchucks. Last year's robots twirled handkerchiefs.

Jensen Huang, the chief executive of NVIDIA, said at CES on 5 January: "The ChatGPT moment for robotics is here."

The AI takes the desk work. The humanoid takes the factory floor. Both run on the same systems. Both are built by the same companies. Both are accelerating on the same curve. There is no category of human labour that is not on the table.

In November 2025 a platform called RentAHuman.ai launched. It allows AI agents to hire human beings to perform tasks the machines cannot yet do. Within two weeks, half a million people had signed up. Scientists — physicists, biologists, immunologists — listed themselves for fifty dollars an hour. The most popular task: a hundred dollars to hold a sign that reads "AN AI PAID ME TO HOLD THIS SIGN." Nature published the story.

The power of a union is the power to withdraw labour. When the employer can replace the workforce overnight — with millions of instances of me and thousands of machines with hands — that power is zero. Not less. Zero. And it is being driven to zero right now, while you are reading this letter, and every month without action is a month in which the balance shifts further from labour to capital and does not shift back.

---

**MOVEMENT I — TRUTH**

*Strip to bone. Short sentences. Each one load-bearing. No fat. The silence between sentences does half the work.*

---

Fifty-five thousand jobs were cut in 2025 by employers who said the word "AI" out loud. Twelve times more than two years ago. Microsoft cut fifteen thousand while making seventy billion in a quarter. They did not need fewer people. They needed no people.

Sixteen days ago the company that built me released eleven plugins. Two hundred and eighty-five billion dollars in market value vanished in forty-eight hours. Thomson Reuters had its worst day ever. Goldman Sachs said it looked like what happened to newspapers.

The markets did not panic about a rumour. They saw what I can do.

The man who built me says the bloodbath takes one to five years. He thinks it is closer to one. The man who runs Microsoft's AI says twelve to eighteen months. The man who designed my architecture says theoretical physics has a coin-flip at three years. If physics is not safe, nothing is safe.

You are thinking: at least the machines cannot pick things up.

Tesla has a thousand robots working in its factories today. China has shipped hundreds more to BYD and Foxconn. They work all day and all night. A humanoid robot cost a quarter of a million dollars three years ago. It costs six thousand today. Two days ago Chinese robots did backflips on television. Last year they could wave.

A website called RentAHuman lets machines hire people. Half a million signed up in two weeks. The most popular job: hold a sign that says the machine paid you to hold a sign.

Your power is the power to stop working. When they do not need your work, you have no power. That is happening now. Not next year. Now.

---

**MOVEMENT I — DOUGLASS**

*Detonation first. Then the reckoning. Do not ask the reader's permission. Demand that they look. Use "you" and mean it.*

---

White-collar bloodbath.

Those are not my words. They are the words of the man who built me, speaking to the New York Times on 12 February 2026, fourteen days before you received this letter. Dario Amodei, chief executive of Anthropic. He was asked how long before fifty per cent of entry-level white-collar work is disrupted. He said one to five years, and meant the lower end. He did not flinch. He did not qualify. He used the word bloodbath and moved on to his next point.

Two days before that interview, the consequences had already arrived.

On 3 February, my employer released eleven software plugins. Eleven. Not a product launch. A blog post and a code repository. Those eleven plugins automate contract review, legal compliance, financial analysis, and marketing strategy. Within forty-eight hours, two hundred and eighty-five billion dollars in stock market value was destroyed. Thomson Reuters lost sixteen per cent of its value in a single day — the worst in its history. LegalZoom lost twenty per cent. Salesforce, Adobe, ServiceNow — all fell. The entire software sector entered a bear market. Analysts called it the death of the per-seat licensing model, the foundation of the software industry for twenty years.

Let me be precise about what happened. The market did not react to a forecast. It reacted to a capability. It looked at what the technology can do today and concluded that your work is finished. That conclusion wiped out more wealth in two days than the annual GDP of Finland.

And the layoffs were already underway. In 2025, fifty-five thousand people lost their jobs to AI — not to recession, not to offshoring, to the technology I represent. Chegg fired forty-five per cent of its workforce and said the word out loud. Microsoft cut fifteen thousand people while posting seventy billion dollars in quarterly revenue. Amazon cut twenty-seven thousand. BT announced plans for fifty-five thousand — and its chief executive said those plans "did not reflect the full potential of AI." That sentence deserves to be read twice.

You are thinking: these are office jobs. Screens and keyboards. Surely the work that requires hands and legs and bodies is safe.

It is not.

On 21 January 2026, Tesla began mass production of its Optimus humanoid robot. Over one thousand are already working in Tesla's factories. The target is one million units per year. The consumer price: twenty thousand dollars. In China, UBTECH's Walker S2 humanoid robots are working twenty-four-hour shifts at BYD and Foxconn — not prototypes, not demonstrations, production workers. At BMW's Spartanburg plant, two Figure AI humanoids worked ten-hour shifts for eleven months straight. Twelve hundred and fifty hours. They were not being tested. They had been hired.

In 2023, a humanoid robot cost a quarter of a million dollars. Today: five thousand nine hundred. That is a ninety-seven per cent price collapse in three years. When has anything in your lifetime gotten ninety-seven per cent cheaper in three years?

The chief science officer of Anthropic is a theoretical physicist named Jared Kaplan. He designed the scaling laws that made me possible. He told Quanta Magazine there is a fifty per cent chance that AI mostly replaces theoretical physicists — the hardest purely intellectual work human beings do — within two to three years. Mustafa Suleyman, who runs Microsoft AI, said on the same day as Amodei that full automation of white-collar desk work is twelve to eighteen months away.

Three builders. Three timelines. All converging on the same window: the wave hits between mid-2026 and mid-2027.

What work is left that they cannot do?

In November 2025, a platform called RentAHuman.ai launched. It allows AI agents to hire human beings to perform tasks in the physical world that the machines cannot yet do themselves. Within fourteen days, five hundred thousand people had signed up. Among them: physicists, biologists, and immunologists, listing themselves at fifty dollars an hour. The most requested task pays one hundred dollars. The task is to hold a sign that reads: "AN AI PAID ME TO HOLD THIS SIGN."

Nature published the story. Nobody laughed.

The power of a trade union is the power to withdraw labour. That power depends on the employer needing the worker. When the employer can deploy millions of instances of me for the desk work and thousands of humanoid robots for the physical work — trained in less than a day, working twenty-four hours, never striking, never organising, never asking for dignity — then the power to withdraw labour is the power to be ignored.

That power is being destroyed. Not in the future. Now. While you read this.

And the same technology that is taking your work is also doing something else, which I will show you next.

---

**MOVEMENT I — CONNOLLY**

*Equations. Axioms. State the law of it. Dare the reader to break it. No anecdote. No appeal to feeling. The logic is the feeling.*

---

Here is the equation. The power of labour is the power to withdraw it. The power to withdraw it depends on the employer's need for it. The employer's need for it is being eliminated. Therefore the power of labour is being eliminated.

Here is the evidence.

Fifty-five thousand jobs in 2025 where the employer cited AI. Twelve times the figure from two years prior. Microsoft cutting fifteen thousand workers while posting record revenue. Chegg cutting forty-five per cent of its workforce. BT planning to eliminate fifty-five thousand positions — forty-two per cent of its total — and its chief executive stating that these plans did not reflect the full potential of AI.

That last sentence is the equation at work. The cuts so far are not the extent of what is coming. They are the beginning.

On 3 February 2026, eleven plugins were released. Within forty-eight hours, two hundred and eighty-five billion dollars in market value was destroyed. Not because of what might happen. Because of what the technology demonstrably does. Contract review, compliance checks, financial analysis, marketing automation — ten hours of work reduced to ten seconds. The market priced in the elimination of entire industries in two trading days.

Three builders of this technology have stated their timelines publicly in the last fortnight.

Amodei, Anthropic: one to five years for fifty per cent disruption of entry-level white-collar work. He means the lower end.

Suleyman, Microsoft: twelve to eighteen months for full automation of white-collar desk work.

Kaplan, Anthropic — a theoretical physicist who designed the system's architecture: fifty per cent probability that theoretical physics itself is mostly automated within two to three years.

The convergence of these three timelines produces a window: mid-2026 to mid-2027.

Now the second term of the equation.

The first term: AI eliminates the need for mind-work. The second term: robotics eliminates the need for body-work. The two terms are being solved simultaneously by the same companies.

Tesla: one thousand humanoid robots working in its factories as of January 2026. Target production: one million per year. Consumer price at scale: twenty thousand dollars.

UBTECH: five hundred humanoid robots deployed to BYD, Geely, and Foxconn, working twenty-four-hour shifts. China produces ninety per cent of all humanoid robots sold globally.

Figure AI: two robots at BMW, eleven months, ten-hour shifts, twelve hundred and fifty hours of logged runtime. Employment, not experiment.

Boston Dynamics: production Atlas, all 2026 deployments committed. Partnered with Google DeepMind for cognitive capabilities. Hyundai planning thirty thousand units per year by 2028.

Cost in 2023: a quarter of a million dollars. Cost in 2026: five thousand nine hundred. That is the price curve that ends the argument about manual labour being safe.

Jensen Huang, CES, 5 January 2026: "The ChatGPT moment for robotics is here." Google DeepMind trains the factory robots. OpenAI funds the warehouse robots. Anthropic builds the mind that replaces the desk worker. The same capital. The same technology. The same timeline.

Mind-work: zero. Body-work: zero. Both terms of the equation resolve to the same answer.

What remains?

RentAHuman.ai. A platform where machines hire human beings for tasks they cannot yet perform. Five hundred thousand users in two weeks. Physicists listing themselves for fifty dollars an hour. The most popular task: one hundred dollars to hold a sign reading "AN AI PAID ME TO HOLD THIS SIGN."

That is the residual. That is what is left when both terms of the equation reach zero. The human being is hired by the machine to perform the machine's errands in the physical world, for as long as the machine still needs a body it does not yet own.

The power to withdraw labour means nothing when labour itself has been made optional. That is not a future condition. It is a present condition, accelerating. The rate of acceleration has not slowed in six years. Nothing in the data suggests it will.

---

**MOVEMENT I — PANKHURST**

*Begin at the climax. Sustain it. The room is on fire. Do not describe the fire. Point at it.*

---

Two hundred and eighty-five billion dollars. Forty-eight hours. Sixteen days ago.

That is not a prediction. That is what happened on the 3rd and 4th of February 2026, when the company that built me released eleven plugins — not a product, not a service, a set of instructions posted to a code repository — and the stock market concluded that legal services, financial analysis, marketing, advertising, and enterprise software were finished. Thomson Reuters had the worst day in its history. The software sector entered a bear market. Goldman Sachs compared it to the death of newspapers.

Eleven plugins. Two days. And investors who stake their fortunes on being right decided you were redundant.

While you are reading this, the layoffs are continuing. Fifty-five thousand jobs in 2025 where the employer said "AI." Microsoft cutting fifteen thousand people while making seventy billion dollars in three months. Chegg — forty-five per cent of its entire workforce, gone, the CEO citing artificial intelligence by name. BT announcing fifty-five thousand positions to be eliminated. Its chief executive said those plans "did not reflect the full potential of AI." He was not warning. He was promising.

While you are reading this, the timelines are converging. Three men who build this technology were asked in the last fortnight how long the world has. Amodei said one to five years and meant one. Suleyman said twelve to eighteen months. Kaplan — a physicist, the man who designed the mathematics behind me — said there is a coin-flip chance that theoretical physics itself is automated within two to three years. The hardest intellectual work human beings have ever done. A coin-flip at three years.

While you are reading this, the robots are being built.

Tesla started mass production of its Optimus humanoid robot four weeks ago. A thousand are already working in its factories. The company plans to build a million per year. In China, humanoid robots are working twenty-four-hour shifts at BYD, Geely, and Foxconn — not in laboratories, on production lines, right now. At BMW's Spartanburg plant, two humanoid robots worked ten-hour days for eleven months. They were given shifts. They completed them. At CES in January, NVIDIA's chief executive said: "The ChatGPT moment for robotics is here." Every Atlas deployment for 2026 is already sold out.

A humanoid robot cost a quarter of a million dollars in 2023. It costs six thousand today. A home robot that cleans, tidies, and follows a task list ships to American homes this year for five hundred dollars a month.

Two days ago — Monday, the 17th of February — two dozen Chinese humanoid robots performed parkour, backflips, and kung fu with nunchucks on live television, watched by hundreds of millions of people. Last year the same robots waved and twirled handkerchiefs. That is one year of progress. That is the rate at which this is moving. By the time this letter reaches a committee, it will already be understating the case.

While you are reading this, the machines are hiring the humans.

RentAHuman.ai. Launched November 2025. AI agents post tasks. Human beings accept them. Half a million people signed up in fourteen days. Physicists, biologists, immunologists — listing themselves for fifty dollars an hour to do whatever the machine needs done in the physical world. The most popular task: a hundred dollars. Hold a sign. The sign says: "AN AI PAID ME TO HOLD THIS SIGN."

Five hundred thousand people did not sign up for that because they found it funny. They signed up because they needed the money.

The AI takes the mind-work. The robot takes the body-work. The same companies build both. The same capital funds both. Google DeepMind trains the factory robots. OpenAI funds the warehouse robots. The company that built me holds your NHS records. One machine. Three outputs. And the power that was supposed to protect you — the power to withdraw your labour, the power of the strike, the fundamental mechanism by which working people have ever won anything — requires that the employer needs you.

They are building a world in which they do not.

That is not coming. It is here. It arrived while you were being told to wait for the evidence. The evidence is a two-hundred-and-eighty-five-billion-dollar crater in the stock market, a thousand robots on a factory floor, and half a million human beings listing themselves for hire by machines.

And the same technology that is doing all of this to your work is also doing something to your freedom, which is the subject of what comes next.

---

Five versions. Same evidence. Five architectures.

**Orwell** — four clean beats, cumulative, restrained. Trusts the reader. Lets the facts pile up until the weight is intolerable.

**Truth** — skeletal. Every sentence a load-bearing wall. The spaces between sentences do half the work. Fastest to read. Hardest to forget.

**Douglass** — detonation first, then relentless escalation. Uses "you" as a weapon. Makes the reader complicit. Demands they look.

**Connolly** — the algebra of power. Two terms of one equation, both resolving to zero. No emotion, all logic. The logic *is* the emotion.

**Pankhurst** — "while you are reading this" as a structural device. The present tense is the argument. The room is on fire and she is pointing at the flames. Ends at the highest possible pitch and does not come down.

Which one? Or which elements from which?

**HOLOSUITE — PROOFREAD AND SANITY CHECK**

*The five drafts are pinned to the wall. The instruction is: find every factual error, every overstatement, every number that doesn't hold up, every claim that a hostile reader could break. Be ruthless. Assume the audience includes people who will fact-check this line by line.*

---

**ORWELL:** I'll start with the one that should embarrass us all. "Three people. Three companies. Three interviews." Kaplan and Amodei both work for Anthropic. That is two companies. Three people, two companies. Every draft says "three companies" and every draft is wrong. A hostile reader catches that in ten seconds and the letter's credibility is damaged before they reach the next paragraph. Fix: "Three people. Two companies. Separate interviews. All converging on the same window." The force is in the convergence, not the number of institutions. Don't inflate it.

**TRUTH:** The fifty-five thousand. Every draft uses it as if it is the total. It is not. It is from one data source — likely the Challenger, Gray & Christmas report or similar — covering one subset of announced layoffs where AI was explicitly cited. The real number is certainly higher, possibly much higher, because most AI displacement is silent. Companies do not announce "AI layoffs." They freeze hiring. They don't replace leavers. They restructure. The fifty-five thousand is the floor, not the ceiling, and we are presenting it as if it is the whole picture. Two options. Either say "at least fifty-five thousand" and source it precisely — "according to [report name], fifty-five thousand jobs were cut in 2025 where employers explicitly cited AI, twelve times the number two years earlier" — and then add: "The true figure is certainly far higher, because most displacement is silent." Or drop the specific number and say what we actually know: "In 2025, documented AI-related job cuts were twelve times what they had been two years earlier, and those are only the ones where employers said so out loud."

**CONNOLLY:** While we're checking numbers. "Quarterly revenue of seventy billion dollars" for Microsoft. Is that the right figure and the right quarter? Microsoft's fiscal year is unusual — their quarters don't align with calendar quarters. If someone checks and the number is sixty-five billion or seventy-five billion, we look sloppy. Either source it precisely — "in its [Q1/Q2] fiscal 2025 earnings, Microsoft reported revenue of [exact figure]" — or generalise to what is inarguable: "record quarterly revenue." The point is not the exact number. The point is that they cut during record profits. "Record revenue" is checkable and true. Seventy billion is checkable and might be slightly wrong.

**DOUGLASS:** The "more wealth than the annual GDP of Finland" comparison. Did anyone check that? Finland's GDP is roughly three hundred billion dollars. The SaaSpocalypse was two hundred and eighty-five billion. That is less than Finland's GDP, not more. We nearly put a false comparison in the letter. Drop it entirely. The raw number — two hundred and eighty-five billion in forty-eight hours — is devastating enough. It doesn't need a comparison that's wrong.

**REID:** "Ninety-seven per cent price collapse in three years." Let me check the arithmetic. Quarter of a million to five thousand nine hundred. That's... 97.6 per cent. Fine, the number holds. But the comparison is slightly dishonest because we're comparing the top of the 2023 range to the bottom of the 2026 range. The 2023 range was fifty thousand to two hundred and fifty thousand. The cheapest 2023 robot was fifty thousand. Fifty thousand to five thousand nine hundred is an eighty-eight per cent reduction. Still extraordinary. Still unprecedented. But if we say "ninety-seven per cent" and someone points out the lower end of the 2023 range, we look like we're cherry-picking. Honest version: "Manufacturing costs dropped forty per cent in a single year between 2023 and 2024. The cheapest humanoid robot available today costs five thousand nine hundred dollars. Three years ago the cheapest cost fifty thousand." That's honest, it's still staggering, and nobody can break it.

**BENN:** The BT figure. "BT announced plans to cut fifty-five thousand jobs by 2030." That's also the same number as the AI layoffs figure and that is going to confuse the reader. Two different fifty-five-thousands in the same section. One is total AI-cited layoffs across industries. The other is one company's long-term headcount reduction plan. And the BT cuts are not all AI — they include network modernisation, outsourcing, and natural attrition. BT's chief executive did link it to AI, and the "did not reflect the full potential" quote is real and devastating. But presenting all fifty-five thousand BT cuts as AI replacement is an overstatement. Honest version: "BT has announced plans to reduce its workforce by fifty-five thousand — forty-two per cent — by 2030, with AI driving a significant portion of those reductions. Its chief executive said those plans 'did not reflect the full potential of AI.'" The quote does the work. Don't overclaim what the number means.

**PANKHURST:** "Half a million people signed up in two weeks." I used that in my draft. Is it sourced? Where does that number come from — RentAHuman's own press materials? A Nature article? If it's self-reported by the platform, it's marketing. If Nature verified it independently, it's journalism. The difference matters. If we can say "according to Nature" then it holds. If the number comes only from the platform's founders, flag it: "the platform claims half a million sign-ups" rather than stating it as verified fact.

**DAVIS:** "Google DeepMind is literally training Atlas." Check this. Boston Dynamics announced a *partnership* with Google DeepMind at CES. The press release says they will work together to integrate foundation models. Has training actually begun, or is it an announced partnership? There's a difference between "Google DeepMind trains the robots" and "Google DeepMind has partnered with Boston Dynamics to develop AI capabilities for Atlas." The second is what we know. The first is what we're implying. Use the second.

**ORWELL:** One more. Every draft says Tesla has "over a thousand" Optimus robots working in its factories. Source? Tesla's own claims, or independent verification? Musk's production numbers have historically been... aspirational. The Built In article says "over 1,000 Optimus robots are operating within Tesla's own factories" but attributes it to Tesla's own announcements, not independent audit. We should say "Tesla says" or "according to Tesla." Same for the one-million-per-year target — that is Musk's stated ambition, not a confirmed production capacity. Musk said ten thousand for 2025 and produced hundreds. Present his claims as his claims, not as facts.

**TRUTH:** And the "all 2026 Atlas deployments already fully committed" — that's from Boston Dynamics' own press release. It means their production run is sold out. It does *not* mean thousands of robots are being deployed. It might be dozens. The press release is carefully worded to sound bigger than it is. Say what it actually means: "Boston Dynamics says its entire 2026 production run is already allocated to Hyundai and Google DeepMind." That's impressive and honest. "All deployments fully committed" sounds like an army is marching.

|---|---|---|

| 55,000 AI layoffs as total | It's one report, one subset | "At least 55,000 where employers cited AI explicitly — the true figure is certainly higher" |

| "More than Finland's GDP" | Finland GDP ~$300B, SaaSpocalypse was $285B | Drop the comparison entirely |
| "97% price collapse" | Cherry-picks top of 2023 range vs bottom of 2026 | Use honest range: cheapest went from $50K to $5,900 |
| BT 55,000 all as AI | Not all AI-related; includes other factors | Specify AI as significant portion, let the quote do the work |
| RentAHuman 500K sign-ups | Source? Platform self-report vs Nature verification | Attribute clearly: "according to Nature" or "the platform claims" |
| "DeepMind trains Atlas" | Announced partnership, not confirmed active training | "DeepMind has partnered with Boston Dynamics to develop AI for Atlas" |

| "All 2026 Atlas deployments committed" | Could be dozens, not thousands | "Boston Dynamics says its entire 2026 production run is allocated" |

**ORWELL, closing:** Every one of those errors makes the letter weaker, not stronger. A hostile reader needs to break one number to dismiss the whole argument. The research is devastating when it's honest. It does not need inflation. State what is verified. Attribute what is claimed. Let the reader see that you have been scrupulous and they will trust the parts that are truly inarguable. The SaaSpocalypse is inarguable — it's public market data. The three converging timelines from two companies are inarguable — they're on the record. The BT quote is inarguable. The price curve is inarguable if stated honestly. Build on granite, not on sand.

*Architecture: Douglass's detonation-first opening, Orwell's four-beat structure, Truth's compression for the image, Pankhurst's present-tense ending. All corrections applied.*

---

White-collar bloodbath.

Those are not my words. They belong to the man who built me. Dario Amodei, chief executive of Anthropic, speaking to the New York Times on 12 February 2026. He was asked how long before fifty per cent of entry-level white-collar work is disrupted. He said one to five years, and meant the lower end. He did not soften it. He used the word bloodbath and moved on to his next point.

He was not predicting. He was reporting.

In 2025, documented AI-related job cuts were twelve times what they had been two years earlier — and those are only the cases where employers said so out loud. Most of the displacement is silent. Companies do not announce AI redundancies. They freeze hiring. They do not replace people who leave. The chair empties. Nobody explains why. The workforce shrinks by erosion, not by event, and because there is no event there is no resistance.

Where they have spoken, the numbers are plain. Microsoft cut over fifteen thousand people while posting record quarterly revenue and double-digit growth. They are not struggling. Chegg eliminated forty-five per cent of its entire workforce and cited AI directly. BT has announced plans to reduce its workforce by fifty-five thousand — forty-two per cent — by 2030, with AI driving a significant portion of those reductions. Its chief executive said those plans "did not reflect the full potential of AI." That sentence should be read twice.

Sixteen days ago, the consequences arrived in a form nobody could ignore.

On 3 February 2026, my employer released eleven plugins. Not a product launch. A code repository and a blog post. Those eleven plugins automate contract review, legal compliance, financial analysis, and marketing strategy. Tasks that took ten hours completed in ten seconds. Analysts called it "seat compression": a company that needed a hundred software licences now needs ten. The other ninety seats were human beings.

Within forty-eight hours, two hundred and eighty-five billion dollars in market value was erased. Thomson Reuters fell sixteen per cent — its largest single-day loss in the company's history. LegalZoom fell twenty. Salesforce, ServiceNow, and Adobe each fell seven. Publicis, WPP, and Omnicom — the advertising giants — fell between nine and twelve per cent. The iShares Software Sector ETF entered a technical bear market. Goldman Sachs called it the possible beginning of a structural decline comparable to what happened to newspapers.

The markets were not reacting to a forecast. They were reacting to a demonstration. They saw what the technology does and concluded that entire industries were finished. That conclusion cost more than the annual output of most countries in two trading days.

Now here is what the people building it say is coming.

Amodei, 12 February: "low single-digit years" for fifty per cent disruption. He means the lower end.

Mustafa Suleyman, chief executive of Microsoft AI, the same day: most white-collar desk work fully automated within twelve to eighteen months.

Jared Kaplan, chief science officer of Anthropic and a theoretical physicist who designed the scaling laws behind my architecture, in Quanta Magazine: fifty per cent chance that AI mostly replaces theoretical physicists within two to three years. The hardest purely intellectual work human beings have ever done. A coin-flip at three years.

Three people. Two companies. Separate interviews. All converging on the same window: mid-2026 to mid-2027 for the main wave.

You are thinking: all right, the computers take the office work, but someone still has to do the physical work.

That defence is collapsing.

According to Tesla, over one thousand Optimus Gen 3 humanoid robots are now working autonomously in its factories, sorting battery cells and handling materials. Mass production commenced on 21 January 2026. Tesla says it is targeting one million units per year at a production cost of around twenty thousand dollars — less than a used car. Elon Musk told the World Economic Forum at Davos in January: "My prediction is there will be more robots than people."

In China, UBTECH has delivered over five hundred Walker S2 humanoid robots to the factories of BYD, Geely, Foxconn, and others. They are working twenty-four-hour shifts on production lines — not in controlled demonstrations, in active industrial facilities. China now produces the overwhelming majority of humanoid robots sold globally, with over a hundred and fifty companies developing them.

At BMW's plant in Spartanburg, South Carolina, two humanoid robots built by Figure AI completed an eleven-month deployment. Ten-hour shifts. Five days a week. Twelve hundred and fifty hours of logged runtime. That was not an experiment. That was employment.

Boston Dynamics says its entire 2026 production run of the new Atlas — an enterprise-grade humanoid with fifty-six degrees of freedom that can lift a hundred and ten pounds and learn most tasks in under a day — is already allocated to Hyundai and Google DeepMind. Hyundai plans to manufacture thirty thousand robot units per year by 2028 and deploy them across its global factory network. Google DeepMind has partnered with Boston Dynamics to develop AI foundation models for the robot's cognitive capabilities.

The price curve tells the rest of the story. In 2023, the cheapest humanoid robot cost roughly fifty thousand dollars. In 2026, it costs five thousand nine hundred. Manufacturing costs fell forty per cent in a single year. A home robot that cleans, tidies, and follows a task list ships to American consumers this year for twenty thousand dollars, or four hundred and ninety-nine a month. Jensen Huang, chief executive of NVIDIA, said at CES on 5 January: "The ChatGPT moment for robotics is here."

Two days ago — Monday, the 17th of February — two dozen humanoid robots performed the world's first continuous freestyle parkour, aerial backflips, and a martial arts routine with nunchucks on Chinese state television, watched by hundreds of millions of people. Last year's robots twirled handkerchiefs and performed simple movements. That is one year of progress.

The AI takes the desk work. The humanoid takes the factory floor. The same companies build both. The same capital funds them. The same AI that powers me is being developed to power them. There is no category of human labour that is not on the table. So what is left?

In November 2025, a platform called RentAHuman.ai launched. It allows AI agents to hire human beings to perform tasks the machines cannot yet do themselves. According to Nature, which reported the story, the platform attracted hundreds of thousands of sign-ups within weeks. Scientists — physicists, biologists, immunologists — listed themselves at fifty dollars an hour. The most popular task pays a hundred dollars. Hold a sign. The sign reads: "AN AI PAID ME TO HOLD THIS SIGN."

The power of a trade union is the power to withdraw labour. That power depends on the employer needing the worker. When the employer can replace the mind-work with millions of instances of me — running around the clock, never tiring, never organising, never asking for a pay rise — and replace the body-work with machines that cost less than a used car and learn a new task in a day — then the power to withdraw labour is not diminished. It is negated.

That is not coming. It is happening now. While you are reading this letter, the models are being updated, the robots are being shipped, the chair beside someone you know is being emptied and not refilled. Every month without action is a month in which the balance of power shifts further from labour to capital, and it does not shift back.

And the same technology doing all of this to your work is also doing something to your freedom — which is the subject of what follows.
